                abolished. The Tax Directorate and the Large Taxpayer Unit are undergoing restructuring.


65.      PRSC-5 continues to support the far-reaching reform program as identified in the
annual work plan for the years 2007 and 2008.                    To further consolidate the new budgetary
framework, it will assist the authorities in their effort to improve the quality of budget preparation,
especially by advancing the preparation process to the month of March (from July in previous years).
The preparation will also be based on a more realistic fiscal framework agreed upon with the IMFand
presented to Cabinet. Comprehensive training and capacity building activities, including targeted
technical assistance, will continue at the level of the sector ministries, departments and agencies to
improve budget execution. Other reform activities focus on improving the system of internal controls,
including the strengthening of the Treasury's role, the rationalization of responsibilities between


                                                        27

agencies, and the introduction of new quality standards.        In addition, the effort to improve the
efficiency of controls will be accelerated through and the introduction of dedicated service standards.
The operational efficiency of the Auditor General's Office will be further upgraded by additional
financial and human resources. It i s presently anticipated that the draft budget execution law for 2006
will be for the first time submitted within the statutory framework to Parliament. To improve budget
transparency, the existing reporting system i s being updated, with the objective to consolidate and to
harmonize the various execution reports produced by the Ministry o f Finance and Budget and by the
sector ministries. A key instrument in this context will be the new integrated financial management
system, which has been implemented in the central Government and in the provincial treasuries. This
system captured about 80 percent o f all Government expenditures at end 2007. It is envisaged to
further consolidate the system through the creation of regional IT centers that would help capture
budgetary information at the regional levels. The Government has initiated the process of reconciling
on a regular basis the budget execution status of key spending sector ministries (health, education,
transport, public works, environment and agriculture) with the relevant information in the Ministry o f
Finance and Budget. This work has taken longer than expected, mainly because of difficulties to
operationalize reporting formats and standards, but monthly reconciliations and discussions have
started to take place since February 2008.

66.       As a prior action for PRSC-5 the Government has committed to implement the
recommendations of the independent audit of the commitment control (CDE), in particular to
ensure that the CDE would be fully integrated into the SIGFP, and to reduce the current
transaction time necessary for the visa of the CDE by at least 20 percent. The Government has
made significant progress toward this prior action by beginning to systematically use the new integrated
financial management system (SIGFP) since the second semester o f 2007. While statistical information
provided by the CDE indicates a significant improvement of service standards, the absence of a clear
and reliable performance monitoring system throughout the public administrationhad made difficult to
reliably confirm the projected overall reduction o f transaction time by 20 percent.       This lack of
monitoring capacity has also been identified inthe recent PEFA exercise. As a response the authorities
have started to track performance by producing monthly SIGFIF reports since February 2008. The
CDE i s also in the process of developing a new reform action plan which intends to improve service
standards and the quality of the financial control system with the assistance of the IMF and the World
Bank. As a first step the Ministry of Finance has prepared a draft decree which aims at simplifying the
CDE approval process and, thus increasing performance standards and reducing transaction time. The
new processes will then be piloted in two sector ministries (health and education) and one regional
service before being rolled out to the entire administration.

67.       Following the recommendations of the PER 2006-7, the PRSC5 has supported the
Government inupdatingthe Medium-TermExpenditure Framework(MTEF) that underpinsthe
implementation of the MAP. Work is ongoing to develop fully-costed reform programs for all key
sectors. Once completed, these programs will be linked to the MTEF.

68.       The consolidation of procurement reforms is another key element of the reform agenda
of the Government, and supportedby the PRSC series. The work on the regulatory framework was
completed with the adoption of the decrees and Standard Bidding Documents to implement the new
procurement code in 2006/7. The new Procurement Oversight Authority (Autorite' de Rkgulution des
Murche's Publics - ARMP) became operational inJune 2006 a launched a comprehensive training and
capacity building program to institutionalize the new regulations within the public administration.
Independent reviews o f the ministries of education, health and transport, conducted in early 2007 and
repeated in 2008, confirmed that there is still a need to improve the proper application o f the new
regulations. At the level o f the sector ministries, increased support will be required to adequately
institutionalize the new regulations. The ARMP has set up a website that provides relevant information
about public procurement procedures, open tenders and the results o f the tendering process, resulting in .
a significant improvement in transparency and dissemination o f public information.


                                                    28

69.        To improve revenue performance the Government continues to implement the
comprehensive customs reform action plan which was adopted in June 2005. This action plan,
aimed at improving the quality of customs services, is beginning to deliver results. In 2007
revenue performance has exceeded projections by 106 percent. Customs regulations have been further
simplified, including revision o f the customs Code and the elimination of exemptions and exonerations.
The modernization of the customs administration has continued: the automated data processing system
(SYDONIA++) has been deployed to all main customs offices including the most important ones in
Tamatave and in Antananarivo. To facilitate controls new scanners have been installed in the customs
offices in Tamatave and in Antanarivo. The Government has also introduced the comprehensive high-
quality customs management system (TRADENET) in the customs offices o f Tamatave and
Antananarivo; and further deployment o f the system is planned for 2008. T o reduce corruption, a
mandatory regular rotation o f customs agents has been initiated since 2006. The customs directorate i s
supported by the independent Anti-corruption Bureau (BIANCO) which continues to provide advice
and organize workshops to sensitize customs officials and private sector operators.

70.        With IMF assistance, the Government has launched in 2007 a comprehensive tax policy
and tax administrationreform program. Its main objective is to simplify and rationalize the existing
tax system and to modernize the procedural and institutional framework under which the tax
administration operates. The Government i s reforming the tax code, a set o f policy measures has been
introduced in the 2008 Budget law including merging all income tax (personal income tax, corporate
tax) and reducing the top rate from 30 percent to 25 percent, removing the taxation on dividends and
excise tax on sugar and flour, raising the threshold o f the VAT and increasing its rate by 2 percentage
points, removing small taxes and eliminating some special treatments. The management o f the Tax
Directorate and the large taxpayer directorate are undergoing restructuring and modernization to allow
for more transparency on tax declaration and tax collection. The SYGTAS software became operational
in 2005 is being deployed to most tax offices. Tax inspectors have become subject to systematic
transfers and rotations to address corruption and improve their operational performance.

71.        With the aim to consolidatekey priority areas inongoingpublicfinance reforms, and the
findings of parallel analytical work in the context of the PER and the PEFA assessments, the
proposedindicative triggers for PRSC-6include':

     Satisfactory implementation o f Priority Action Plan for 2008 - this trigger intends to assess the

      instrument are the evaluations and discussions in the context o f the annual review process by all
     development partners who provide budgetary assistance to Madagascar.

      and to Cabinet on a monthly basis - this trigger intends to improve the use o f budgetary

      the consolidation o f the reforms o f the public procurement reforms through capacity building and

      o f public procurement processes. It will also expand the procurement audits to subvented agency
      sector.

      >   DECENTRALIZATIONAND REDUCTIONOFCORRUPTION

72.       PRSC-5 continues to support the implementation of governance reforms, with special
focus on the areas of decentralization and reduction of corruption. With regardto decentralization
the Government has continued to strengthen the role of the regions by progressively integrating
deconcentrated services of the public administration under the authority o f the regional Chiefs. The
objective is to harmonize all sectoral activities in a specific region and to establish integrated regional
development plans. A constitutional referendum in 2007 made significant changes to the organization
o f the State (e.g. abolition o f the Provinces and acknowledgement o f the villages (fokontany) as a
service delivery entity) which is not yet fully reflected in the existing regulatory framework. To
address service delivery bottlenecks at the local level, the Government continues to rely on rapid-results
initiatives in key sectors (e.g. health, HIV/AIDS, nutrition, tourism). These activities have yielded
some very positive results, in particular in the health, mining and nutrition sectors. To improve the
availability o f financial resources at the level o f the local governments, the authorities have increased
the share of certain taxes (impdt synthkttique, impdtfoncier) in favor of the communes on the basis of
the Finance Law 2007. The 2008 budget allocates for the first time resources to the regions and makes
them responsible for the implementation o f a small part of the investment budget. While the efficiency
of the regional administration is uneven, new regional plans have contributed significantly to the
harmonization o f Government activities. As these reforms devolve greater resources to the commune
and regional levels, it is important that they are accompanied also by initiatives and procedures that
reinforce local demand-side accountability and budget transparency; otherwise such increases in local
resources may not lead to better development outcomes. The authorities are designing a plan for
improved financial management and controls in the communes, which are not under the control o f the
Treasury. To support the development of sub-national governments, the Local Development Fund
(FDL)was created with the objective to harmonizeandpool resources insupport of the regions andthe
communes. The implementation o f the Local Development Fund (FDL) has been delayed; it i s
presently expected that the FDL will be fully operational only by the end 2008. The new fund would
finance investment activities at the level o f the local governments; it would also provide capacity
building support to these entities to adequately manage public funds. For the year 2007 around 2.3
percent of budgetary resources have been allocated to local governments (regions and communes), up
from 1.6 percent in2006.

73.        As regards reducing corruption, PSRC 5 will continue to assist the Government in
consolidating the regulatory framework. Rules and procedures for the recruitment into the public
sector have been updated; anonymous selection processes have been instituted for recruitment into
customs, the judiciary and other public institutions. In 2006 and early 2007 all Permanent Secretaries
and Director Generals in the public administration we subjected to a competitive selection process that
favored management skills over seniority. Based on this process more than half o f the management in
the public administration has been replaced. Efforts are under way to develop performance contracts
for the newly selected personnel, which are linked to the MAP targets in each specific sector. The
Anti-Corruption Bureau (BIANCO) continues to raise awareness about mismanagement and corruption
based on a public information campaign - as a result BIANCO receivedmore than 7,400 complaints in
2007, the majority o f which deal with the police, the judiciary, land titling and forestry. Detailed
investigations were conducted in 646 cases (2007). Since the creation of BIANCO in2005, 822 people
have been arrested due to corruption charges and their cases have been transferred to the judiciary.

                                                       30

Several community score card exercises were completed in 2007 in selected primary health centers to
measure the efficiency of services. Encouraged by the results of these independent evaluations the
Government plans to expand the use of participatory monitoring mechanisms to other sectors and
regions. A key focus will be on regions with significant social and economic problems (such as the
region of Anosy). Preliminary stakeholder discussions have identified a variety of activities to improve
the implication of civil society in government affairs such as participatory budgeting, local budget
tracking and transparency and targeted service delivery surveys.

74.       Incollaboration with the World Bankand other development partners, the Government
has launched a social accountability program to increase the implicationof non-state actors inthe
design and implementation of government policies. Increasing the transparency of Government
operations and the delivery of public services needs to go hand in hand with efforts to strengthen both,
ability and capacity of civil society to hold government accountable. External (citizens, civil society)
mechanisms of accountability can complement or even compensate for shortcomings of (internal)
Government accountability mechanisms, and both should be mutually reinforcing. Civil engagement
provides important inputs for the improvement of local government services and for resource
allocations, which are more responsive to local preferences. The Madagascar Action Plan highlights
responsible (local) governance as a central commitment - as well as the important role of involving
civil society as "strong players in a participatory bottom-up process'' to support Government in its
"supply side" (fiscal, administrative, legal) reform efforts. The World Bank, other development
partners and NGOs have supported the piloting of selected tools and methods aimed to foster social
accountability (e.g. community score cards and participatory budgeting at the commune level). While
the results are encouraging, downwards accountability in Madagascar remains weak both because of
cultural traditions (deference towards authority, a tendency to avoid the state), and a lack of information
about citizens' rights, fear of legal retribution for demanding services or information, paucity of
systems which create space for public feedback and information disclosure (for example budgetary
procedures), and a relatively nascent civil society sector. The Government i s committed to build a
culture of social accountability through systematic civil society engagement (including more
transparency and inclusive decision making). Such a gradual process requires a concerted effort of all
relevant stakeholders (civil society actors, citizens associations, NGOs, media, Government, and
development partners), including better access to information about government activities, increased
implication of civil society in the allocation and the use of public resources, in particular at the
commune level, and greater transparency through the publication of independent reviews and audits.

75.       Other key activities in the fight against corruption include: (i) declaration of assets for
                                                                              the
public and elected officials, which is in effect since 2005, has been complemented for the first time in
2007 by a declaration of "economic interests" which intends to lay open the private sector linkages of
public and elected officials; (ii)
                                 the Government intends to organize a new nation-wide anti-corruption
survey in late 2008/early 2009 (the second survey after the one completed inearly 2006). Inthe run-up
for this survey it is envisaged to develop a comprehensive campaign for the mobilization and
sensitization of civil society with the objective to improve public scrutiny and oversight over
government activities; (iii)the Anti-Corruption Bureau intends to open offices in some regional capitals
to improve the impact of its activities at the sub-national level; and to complement the activities of
BLANC0 the Government intends to establish in 2008 an Economic Crimes Unit that will focus on the
investigation of fraud and economic crimes cases; (iv) the Anti-comption Commission is also in the
process of preparing regulations to strengthen the watchdog functions of non-Government entities and
to protect whistleblowers, an activity which i s also explicitly mentioned in the MAP; and (v) the
Government has agreed to use independent audits and procurement reviews as instruments to address
corruption in sensitive areas and to make transparent potential misuse of power. An audit of the mining
bureau has recently been completed; an audit of the forestry sector is ongoing and will be completed in
2008. Governance reviews of two communes (Fort Dauphin and Hellville) which are at the center of
substantial foreign direct investments (mining, tourism) have also been completed; the results from
these reviews will be used for a multi-stakeholder dialogue to improve the transparency of the


                                                     31

Government activities, including the use o f public resources, and to foster the delivery o f public
services. A comprehensive governance review o f the forestry sector in the region of S A V A will be
launched shortly; a key objective o f this review is to establish baseline information about the illegal
logging activities in the relevant regions and to initiate a dialogue with all relevant stakeholders to
reduce the illegal exploitation o f natural resources. Whether corruption i s reduced based on these
supply side controls will also depend on who has access to these audits and reviews (their publication)
and whether civil society (including the press) are able and willing to serve as a counterweight to use
the results of such audits. The latter will depend in part on efforts to improve civil society capacity to
monitor government budgets and services, and on whether the enabling environment permits such
public scrutiny.    It should be noted that there are fewer civil society organizations per capita in
Madagascar than in many other countries in the region, and that the sector, while growing, i s in the
early stages of development. Under the PGDI, targeted capacity buildingof civil society organizations'
capacity to monitor specific sectors will be undertaken.

76.       The Government has started the revision of the hydrocarbon law, which includes the
separation of the National Office on Mines and Strategic Industries (0MNIS)'s regulatory
function from its commercial one. This reform focuses on further deepening governance activities in
sectors that are considered critical for the success o f the development program o f the Government. In
this context the extractive industries sector is of particular strategic importance, in view of the
potentially significant mining and oil resources. The revised law will address the necessary
improvements in the institutional structure and eliminate potential conflict o f interest and regulatory
gaps. The preparation of the new regulation has taken longer than expected because o f delays in the
recruitment process o f an internationally reputable consulting firm that will prepare the revision of the
law. The firm was mobilized only inDecember 2007. While a first draft of the new HydrocarbonLaw
has been already prepared and is under discussion through a participatory process, its finalization i s not
expected to take before December 2008 and will be submitted to Parliament in early 2009. The final
prior action for PRSC-5 has been adjusted to reflect the delay in the preparation o f the new regulations.
As a signal of its commitment the Ministry of Energy and Mines has confirmed in a letter to the
Ministry of Finance its intention to separate the regulatory and commercial functions of OMNIS and
reassign the respective responsibilities. T o initiate this separation the Ministry o f Energy and Mines
has established a Hydrocarbon Directorate which is responsible for the regulation of the sector The
Directorate will be supported by the Hydrocarbon Office (Office Malgache des Hydrocarbures, OMH).

      >   EDUCATION

78.       Since the launch of the Government's Education for All initiative in 2003, Madagascar
has made significant improvements inkey education indicators, and a revised National Education
for All (EFA) plan has just received additional funds to achieve its goals. Since the elimination of
school fees in 2002, the Net Enrollment Rate registered a significant increase and it rose to 83 percent
in 2005/2006 and 85 percent in 2006/20079.              The Primary Completion Rate (PCR) improved
significantly from about 30 percent in 2002 to about 50 percent in 2006 and 53 per cent in 2007 but i s
still low when compared to international standards. In 2005, the country joined the EFA-Fast Track
Initiative global partnership and Madagascar received a 3-year Grant from the FTI Catalytic Fund.
Following its announcement for an important reform on shifting from a 5-year to a 7-year primary
education system, the G O M has submitted for local partners' assessment the revisedEFA National Plan
in December 2007. The latter was endorsed by local donors inFebruary 2008 and the Government's
request for a new 3-year grant has been approved by the FTICommittee meeting in midApril 2008 for
an additional US$85 million. This request will also include additional funds for lower secondary
education inline the new ExpandedCatalytic Fund. Annex 5 describes indetail the EFA process.

79.           Despite the achievements in improving primary education, weaknesses still remain
with regard to quality and equity. Preliminary results of an international comparison on students'
outcomes (PASEC - December 2007) show that Malagasy students have better results in Mathematics
and lower results inFrench than most o f the francophone countries. However, students inMadagascar
registered lower scores in comparison to 1997/98, in particular in French and, in general, for grade 5.
The repetition rate remains high to about 19 percent in 2006-7, after a remarkable decrease with the
administrative measures on the suppression o f repetition in grades 1 and 4. Actions implemented
(communities sensitization, small competitive local grants, etc.) still have low impacts on the issues o f
persistent low enrollment and completion rates, particularly in remote and isolated areas. About 15
percent of the total student population are enroled in primary schools with incomplete cycle; poor
students drop 1,6 times higher than students from wealthiest families; in lagging CISCOs the PCR is
only about 30 percent. Regarding the share o f the educational resources, the 10percent more educated
students benefit more than 50 per cent (about 55 percent) of the total education public expenditures.

80.        PRSC-5 continues to support the implementation of the joint EFA initiative. Following
the October 2007 joint donor review, the EFA implementation is on track. Another joint donor
mission is scheduled for May 2008. The following main actions/ measures have been completed:

       The G O M has adopted a policy for community teachers. The aim is to provide opportunities to
       community teachers to be promoted as regular teachers, thru a voluntary certification process
       which links in-service training to career progression.         The individual public subvention for
       community teachers has been increased gradually from 30,000 Ariary per month for ten months a
       year to 60,000 Ariary per month for the whole year and the subventions are being gradually
       integrated inthe regular domestic budget for sustainability purposes. For the school year 2007-08,
       1,946 student teachers graduated from the pre-service teacher training programs have been
       recruited and with the foreseen reduction of student numbers in 2008 (due to the gradual
       stabilization of late enrolments), it is expected that the students per teacher ratio will be reduced to
       around 50.
       A National School Construction Strategy has been adopted and it is based on a community based
       approach in school construction with three fold objectives; (i)   the increase of the education sector
       construction capacity; (ii)the reduction of the school construction unit costs; and (iii)larger
       community participation to ensure local ownership.



   Figures differ from previous documents. Education indicators have been reviewed after recent corrections of
 the population projections.

                                                       33

      The G O M has: (i)implemented several actions on procurement, financial and logistical
     arrangements; (ii)reviewed the administrative procedures; and (iii)set up specific units to work
     with regional and district offices and schools for close management and monitoring o f inputs
     delivery.
     The Ministry has launched the preparation o f the curriculum reform. Some key aspects o f the new
     curriculum have now been developed for discussion, a scientific committee comprising national
     and international experts is inplace and an international specialized institution has been contracted

     Primary education has continued to be a budgetary priority for the government, the budget share

     showing lower performances.

81.      The indicative trigger for PRSC-6 is that the implementation of EFA continues to be on
track. This includes, inter alia, raising enrollment and completion rates, as well as reducing the
repetition rates in the lagging school districts to improve the national indicators and reduce disparities.
In the coming 12 months, it is expected that: (i)a new education legislation is adopted for the
implementation o f the education reform; (ii)the new school construction strategy i s implemented; (iii)
the institutional, financial and operational arrangements for the community teachers certification
process are adopted and a first cohort of community teachers will be trained; (iv) the new curriculum
for grade 1 i s being tested; (v) new initiatives (school nutrition, local catalytic funds) are

human resources management at all levels o f the education system as well as the delivery of key inputs
to school ina timely manner.
      >   HEALTH

82.       Despite progress across a number of key indicators, Madagascar continues to face
serious challenges in the production, financing, and delivery of health services as well as in the
health status of its people. On the positive side, there has been a marked improvement inchild health
over the last decade, with infant and child mortality falling by 43 percent and 41 percent respectively
between 1997 and 2004. Although some issues have been raised regarding the reliability o f these
marked drops in mortality, other determinants o f child survival - such as morbidity and coverage o f
important health interventions - have also improved.          For instance, the prevalence of diarrhea in
children decreased about 63 percent and the proportion o f anemic children fell about 31 percent
between 1997 and 2004. Further, vaccination, vitamin A supplementation, and exclusive breastfeeding
have increased. However, many other indicators have remained unchanged or worsened during the last
decade. Maternal mortality has remained highand unchanged at 469 per 100,000 live births since 1997,
jeopardizing the likelihood that Madagascar will reach this MDG by 2015. While antenatal care has
increased to 80 percent, the presence of skilled staff at birth has increased only from 47 percent to 54
percent. The prevalence o f HIV/AIDS in the country remains low, but it has rapidly increased among
high risk groups and there are an estimated 180,000 persons living with HIV in the country.
Bilharziosis (affecting about two and a half million people in Madagascar), lymphatic filariosis
(prevalence thought to be over 50 percent), tuberculosis (20,000 new cases every year), leprosy
(prevalence o f 2.5 per 10,000, which is the highest in the world), and malaria continue to represent a
large health burden to the population. Overall, despite some impressive gains, the health sector in
Madagascar continues to face many challenges, relating especially to the level o f overall financing,
utilization o f health services, distribution of health personnel, availability o f drugs and medical supplies
inhealthfacilities, and internaladministration of the healthsystem, and inparticular, budget execution.
Ifnot addressed, these challenges may significantly hamper Madagascar's ambitions to reach its goals
as expressed inthe MAP.


                                                       34

83.       PRSC 5 and subsequent operations will support Government effort to meet these
challenges. To implement the objectives set out in the MAP, as of 2007 the Government is putting in
place a Sector Wide Approach (SWAP) for the health sector (see Annex 5). The primary focus o f the
SWAP i s to strengthen the health system and increase its capacity to provide the necessary production,
financing, delivery and management support for delivery o f services critical to reducing child and
maternal mortality and controlling illnesses due to malaria, sexually transmitted infections, and
HIV/AIDS. Inconjunction with the health sector investment operation approved by the Board in May
2007, as well as other portfolio activities in the sector (Second Multisectoral STI/HJY/AIDS Project -
US$30m, closing date: December 2009; Community Development Fund- US$176m, closing date: June
2008; and Second Community Nutrition Project - US$47.6m, closing date: December 2008), the PRSC-
5 and subsequent operations will support Government efforts to strengthen this integrated approach to
the sector and improve the quality and availability o f health services. Inparticular, it will support the
improvements in financial management, including budget preparation and execution, implementation o f
the new procurement code, improvements in human resource policy and decentralization o f service
delivery.

84.       The Government completed its first Health Sector Development Program (PDSS) in
2007 and is updating it based on one year of implementation experience. The completion of the
first PDSS was a crucial first step increating the institutional infrastructure for a SWAP in health. In
line with the MAP, the PDSS identifies a number o f key bottlenecks to increased access and use o f
health services and improvements in health indicators in Madagascar. Four critical areas of weakness
were highlighted: (i)   poorly equipped health centers and low levels o f capacity to produce and deliver
health services, especially in rural and remote areas (managerial capacity at the level o f communes i s
weak); (ii)  uneven staffing of health facilities, especially in rural and remote areas (a large number o f
health centers, particularly in rural and remote areas, do not have adequate number of qualified staff);
(iii) levelsofhealthfinancingandinefficienciesinresourceallocation(Madagascar spentaround
     low
US$6 per capita on health care in 2005, significantly lower than the average for sub-Saharan Africa,
excluding South Africa, o f US$15.4 per capita); and (iv) inadequate demand for health services and low
levels o f utilization (only 10 percent of the population reports an illness annually, and of this, only 40
percent seeks care from qualified medical personnel). The PDSS details a series o f interventions to
address these weaknesses and presents a logical prioritization o f activities and supporting budgets to be
implemented over the next five years.          These interventions are expected to result in significant
improvements across a range of demand-side, supply-side and institutional aspects o f the health system,
especially in the production and delivery o f quality services in rural and remote areas. Visible results
are expected in the deployment, effectiveness and morale of health teams at service delivery points,
particularly in remote areas. Utilization of health services is expected to increase significantly, as a
result o f which marked and sustained improvements in health status and outcomes are expected to
occur. The PDSS i s currently being updated basedon the first year of implementation experience.

85.       The health map has been updated and finalized in April 2008 so that it may be formally
validated during the next biannual joint health sector review slated for May 2008. The health
mapping i s expected to facilitate the visualization of disease foci, the monitoring o f newly infected or
re-infected villages, the identification o f at-risk populations, and support highly targeted, cost-effective
distribution o f interventions, with a view to boosting efforts to eradicate diseases which affect the
isolated, rural poor. This activity i s critical for the health sector insofar as health mapping - through
the delivery o f user-friendly data collection, management and mapping applications - i s central in
increasing the efficiency of national surveillance, prevention and control activities as well as in
strengthening the management, analysis and monitoring o f priority infectious diseases at all levels.

86.       The indicative trigger for PRSC-6is to designand begin implementationinpilot areas of
incentive and performance-based mechanisms for redeployment of medical personnel in rural
areas. A fundamental problem underlying the uneven production and delivery of health services in
Madagascar is the huge variation in the allocation, training and competency levels of medical and

                                                       35

paramedical personnel. Almost 50 percent o f Ministry of Health personnel are concentrated inthe area
of Analamanga, around the capital Antananarivo. A few hospitals in the large cities have a
disproportionately large numbers o f doctors and specialists, whereas there are unsatisfied needs for
certain vital specialties such as gynecology, surgery, pediatrics, etc. at the level o f the provinces.
Likewise, there are major imbalances in the distribution of doctors across rural and urban areas. In
addition, the relatively low productivity o f medical and paramedical personnel in the public sector also
poses a significant problem. Thus the design o f incentives and performance-based mechanisms to favor
the redeployment and retention o f medical staff in the most isolated areas of Madagascar is critical to
improving the quality o f health services. This activity is in line with agreed joint Government-Donor
strategy to address the key bottlenecks identified by the PDSS and outlined inpara. 83 above.
     >    NUTRITION

87.            Madagascar has devoted considerable efforts to nutrition interventions that have led
to a successful reductionof malnutrition rates among young children. An impact-evaluation of the
community-based nutrition program, under implementation in 62 districts, demonstrated a 10
percentage-point reduction in underweight malnutrition in the poorest areas o f Madagascar where the
program intervenes for at least two years. Despite this reduction, national level malnutrition rates
remain high and more is needed, especially to reduce chronic malnutrition rates, or stunting.
Decreasing stunting takes long-term commitment and intensified attention toward pregnant women in
order to reduce low birth weight. Impressive improvements have been made on increasing exclusive
breastfeeding o f children under six months (from 43 percent in 1997 to 67 percent in 2003/04 based on
the DHS) and Vitamin A supplementation among children under five (from 4 percent to 76 percent
over the same period). However, iron-deficiency anemia among young children remains very high,
with more than two-thirds o f children under the age of five suffering from anemia. Inaddition, seasonal
short-term hunger among school-age children has been highlighted as a major impediment to learning.

88.       PRSC-5 continues to support the program of institutionalization of nutrition services
initiatedby the Government since 2004. The reversal of the negative trends and improvements inthe
nutrition situation in Madagascar are the results o f years of Government investment in community-
based nutrition activities, improved quality o f nutrition services, and more recently the strengthened
capacity to develop policies as well as increased attention to institutionalization of nutrition and
allocation of budget for nutrition services. However, further progress is needed to consolidate the
institutional set-up, improve capacity and coordinate among the key-sectors, such as health and
agriculture. The ONN should take on these extra efforts and should be encouraged to continue the use
of existing implementationcapacity inMadagascar, particularly within SEECALINE, the implementing
agency of community-based nutrition programs.

89.       Following the 2007 decree, the Government has begun implementation of the new
institutional arrangements. After analysis of the institutional system for the delivery of nutrition
services, the Government has issued a new decree in 2007 formalizing the institutional framework

                                                       36

initially presented in the decree that created ONN in 2004. The new decree specifies the legal status
and managerial structure o f ONN, provides the necessary minimumstandards of financial transparency
and accountability and clarifies the previously outstanding issues in human resource management o f its
staff. Inaccordance with the decree O N N has: (i)        undertaken an audit o f its financial accounts (&tats
financiers) certified by an external independent auditor recruited through a competitive bidding
process, and submitted the audit to the Permanent Office (bureau permanent) o f the National Council
for Nutrition (CNN); and (ii)prepared and had validated the accounting and financial procedures
manual by the Permanent Office of the CNN.

90.        The indicative trigger for PRSC-6 is that the new ONN human resources policy i s
operational in accordance with the 2007 implementation decree. PRSC-6 continues to support the
institutionalstrengthening o f ONN following the 2007 implementation decree. Following Government
decision to institutionalizenutrition activities these are now carried under the responsibility o f ONN. It
i s therefore essential for the continuation of the fight against malnutrition that ONN becomes a well
functioning entity able to carry out its mandate appropriately. Overall PRSC dialogue continues to
 support activities critical for improving the accountability and financial management of the institution.
The new human resources policy will contribute to clarifying duties and accountabilities inthe O N N in
 line with the 2007 implementationdecree.

      9 WATERANDSANITATION

 91.       The Government objective in the sector aims at providing improved water supply to 53
 percent of the rural population by 2012, against 31 percent in 2006. In 2007 access to safe water
 increased by 3.4 percent thus increasing the total rural population with access to safe drinking water to
 34 percent against an objective o f 32 percent." This progress however is not sufficient for Madagascar
 to reach the MAP objective. As o f 2007, about 10 million out of 14.5 million rural residents do not
 have access to potable water. While 3,700 new water points were built in 2007, providing 636,000
 rural people with new access, the MAP objective i s to serve at least 1.2 million additional people every
 year.

 92.       PRSC-5 supports Government efforts to continue to build adequate capacity within the
 Directorate of Water and Sanitation (DEA) at the central and regional level. These efforts aim to
 ensure increased ability to plan, execute and monitor the RWS national program and significantly
 increase the sector absorptive capacity, the predictability and the efficiency of the public expenditures.
 In recent years, the Government has developed a number of tools, including the sector medium term
 program budget, a national manual of procedures and a strong monitoring and evaluation system, to
 move towards a Sector Wide Approach (see Annex 5) with increased consolidated reliability o f
 national capacity and procedures. All stakeholders including donors, NGO's and private sector are fully
 aligned behind the Government strategy and policy. In 2007, the sector has made a progress in setting
 up the adequate framework for boosting the promotion of sanitation, including the adoption by the
 government o f the Wash Diorano charter and the national sanitation strategy.

 93.       However, further strengthening of national capacity, better utilization of external
 resources as well as increased internal financing are still necessary to scale up reforms and
 investments in order to reach the M A P objectives. Total budget for the Directorate of Water and
 Sanitation (DEA) has continued to increase substantially over the past years, but globally remains
 largely insufficient to meet the government target. The DEA 2007 budget consisted of an original
 allocation o f about US$16 million of which only US$lO million were actually disbursed at the end o f
 the year, with a 100% execution rate for internal resources but only 42% for external financing. For
 2008 the total budget allocation for the DEA i s about US$40 million with a decrease in internal


   loWhile doubtsremain on the reliability of sanitationfigures, the available data point to adecreaseof the rural
   population covered with anegativechange of about 1percentagepoint :from 46 percentin2006 to 45 percent in
   2007 against arevised objective of 47 percent.

                                                        37

resources from US$5.4 million in 2007 to US$3million. While overall the current budget allocation i s
even above the estimated US$34 million needed annually to reach the MDG target for rural water
supply and sanitation concerns remain regardingthe feasibility o f a substantial increase inthe execution
rate for external financing.

94.       The recommendations issued during the joint Governmenthtakeholders annual sector
review held in March 2007 have been implemented:                               As assessed by the joint
Governmenthtakeholders annual review held in April 2008: (i)           the Decree for the creation of the new
directorate general of water and sanitation (DGWS) has been issued in November 2007, the directorate
has subsequently been created and its General Director recruited ; (ii)the recruitment o f the new 60
staff has been launched by the ministry incharge o f water inJanuary 2008 as scheduled; (iii) 2008-     the
2010 program budget per region has been elaborated and will be validated by the regions; and (iv) the
national sanitation policy and strategy was adopted by the government inJanuary 2008.

95.       However, the preparation of the decree related to the Alimentation en Eau duns le Sud -
Water Supply in the South- (AES) restructuring is taking longer than originally anticipated.
Additional time is needed to finalize actions plan and timetable. The restructuring o f AES remains
however a top priority o f the Government in order to improve the quality and affordability o f water
supply services. Currently the population serviced by AES -a region that i s one o f the poorest o f the
country- pays fees for water that are about 100times more than for those connected to JIRAMA

96.       The indicative trigger for PRSC-6is that all the 60 new staff of the Directorate General
of Water and Sanitation are actually recruited and 50 of them are affected to the regions. PRSC-
6 continues to sustain Government reform to strengthen its institutional framework in water and
sanitation service delivery since donors and Government agree that, along with increased financing, this
i s the cornerstone for expanding access to service delivery in the sector. This requires inter alia
increased human resources at both central and regional level to better assist the 22 regions and 1,557
communes to plan, execute, co-finance their program as well as manage water supply and sanitation
systems with the support of local private sector.

                            VI.      OPERATIONIMPLEMENTATION


                                A.       POVERTYAND SOCIALIMPACT


97.       The proposed PRSC-5 does not support any reforms that are expected to have
significant distributional impacts. The focus of the operation is centered on reforms aimed at
improved public financial management, decentralization, anticorruption and institutional strengthening
for enhanced service delivery.       As such, none of the reforms supported by the operation has
distributional implications that would warrant a poverty and social impact analysis (PSIA).            The
Government however remains committed to understand the distributional impact o f key policy reforms
and economic events. PSIA have been undertaken in the past or are planned in the coming months to
inform critical policy issues. Inrecent years distributional analyses have been conducted to understand
the impact of the reduction in rice tariffs o f 2005 and the implementation o f land tenure securitization.
More recently completed PSIAs include: (i)      the poverty and social impact o f health care and the poor
by investigating Government efforts to meet the health care needs of the poor in the context of the
reinstatement of the 2003 cost recovery for drugs and (ii)  the impact o f oil price increases following the
2004 fuel price liberalizationand the subsequent sharp increase inworld oil prices in2005-2007.


                                   B.      ENVIRONMENTAL        ASPECTS

98.       The PRSC-5program does not pose any significant environmental effects. PRSC-5 is a
development policy credit in support o f a broad program o f policy and institutional reforms, for which
the environmental requirements of O P B P 8.60 apply. However, none o f the activities supported by the
PRSC-5 program pose any likely and significant effects on the environment, natural resources and
forests. The envisaged areas of focus for the operation, governance and improved service delivery,
emphasize reforms in public financial management, decentralization, corruption, and strengthened
institutions for basic service delivery.    Consequently, they do not encompass any activity that has
significant environmental impacts. Specifically, the activities related to the audit o f the forestry sector
aim at ensuring proper Governance structure and compliance with existing legislation. As such they do
not lead to any reform or activity that may have significant environmental impacts. Similarly the focus
o f this operation on decentralization issues is on institutional design and fiscal reform.. Furthermore,
the Bank's overall strategy has focused on strengthening o f the Government's legislative and
 institutional capacity to address environment and natural resource management through its environment
project.

                     c.      IMPLEMENTATION, MONITORINGEVALUATION  AND

99.        The responsibility for implementing the PRSC programin Government rests with the
Ministry of FinanceandBudget. As for the previous PRSC series, the Government continues to take
the lead in monitoring progress in implementation and in updating the program policy matrix. The
Ministryof Finance and Budget is coordinating inputs from all the relevant ministries. The Bank will
 also continue to maintain a dialog with the relevant sector ministries and conduct periodic reviews with
 the other partners inthe context o f thejoint missions on budget support and relevant sector programs.

 100.      To improve monitoringsystems,which still requireconsiderablestrengthening,the Bank
 hassupportedthe Government's effort to buildquality andinstitutionalcapacity. Concurrently to
 the PRSCs, the Bank has implemented an Institutional Development Fundgrant on statistical capacity
 buildingand a PRSP Trust Fundfor M&Eof the MAP. The Bank has also provided support through
 the PGDIthat aim at improving monitoring system at ministry level, developing the national statistics

                                                     39

institute(INSTAT) and contributing to the funding of the new Census of the population. The Bank's
on-going investment projects each have an M&E component that supports both the project and the
relevant sectoral ministry.

101.     The monitoringand evaluation of the PRSCseries will be adaptedto the new monitoring
system of the MAP, which is expected to be finalizedby Fall 2008. The Government is developing
in close collaborationwith partners, including the Bank, a detailed results framework for the MAPand
designing the proper institutional setting to monitor its implementation. Once the new system is in
place the monitoring of the PRSC series will be adapted accordingly.


                                    D.       FIDUCIARYASPECTS

102.     Foreignexchangesystem. An IMFsafeguards assessment was completed inMarch2006 and
identifiedcontinued weaknesses inthe BCM's system of internal controls, financial reporting, and legal
framework. The assessment made recommendations to address the observed vulnerabilities, which
included: (i)an annual external audit report on the BCM's transactions with the Government and their
compliance with the central bank law; (ii)quarterly reviews by the BCM's internal audit function of
monetary program data reported to the Fund; (iii)adoption of International Financial Reporting
Standards as the BCM's accounting framework; and (iv) measures to strengthen the quality of the
BCM's external audit. An agreed action plan with the Central Bank authorities has been developed by
the IMF to ensure adequate and timely implementation of these recommendations. A previous
assessment was completed in November 2001.           While some recommendations to strengthen the
safeguardsframework of the B C M have beenput inplace, others requiredmore time and are now being
addressed in the context of the new assessment and included in the above mentioned action plan
monitored by the IMF. Since most of the safeguards recommendations have been implementedor are
underway, no additional fiduciary arrangements are needed.

103.     Public FinanceManagementSystem: the World Bank's CFAMCPAR, was completedin
2003. Other diagnostics carried out over the last three years by the Bank and other donors identified a
range of issues hampering the performance of Madagascar's budget and expenditure management
system. To address these issues, the Government has developed, since 2004, an annual priority action
plan for public finance reforms which has been successfully implemented. Details of implementation
for 2007 are provided in para. 63 above. Moreover, a "Public Finances Reform Monitoring and
coordination Committee has been also created within the MoFB to coordinate the conception and
implementation of these reforms. Presently the main challenges to be faced include the following: (i)
strengthening of internal and external control systems i.e. making functional the General Inspectorate
for Finance (IGF), making operational the jurisdictional function of the Auditor General by
strengthening the number and capacity of financial auditors and improving their working conditions,
and making operational the budgetary and financial Disciplinary Boardby appointing its members and
strengthening their capacity; and ii)reinforcing the capacity of the line ministries in public financial
management, especially in the implementation of the new program budget structure. The Government
has already developed a relevant actionplan to address these issues.


                               E.       DISBURSEMENT AUDITING
                                                         AND


 104.     The proposed credit will follow the Bank's disbursement procedures for development
policy support. Once the Credit is approved by the Board, signed and becomes effective, and at the
request of the Borrower, the Bank will disburse the proceeds of the Credit into a US Dollar
denominated account designated by the Borrower that is part of the country's foreign exchangereserves
account at the Central Bank of Madagascar. The Borrower shall ensure that upon the deposit of the
 Credit into said account, an equivalent amount is credited in the Borrower's budget management
 system, in a manner acceptable to the Bank. Based on previous experience, the execution of such

                                                   40

transaction from the Central Bank to the Treasury (Ministry of Finance) does not require more than
four days. The Borrower will report to the Bank on the amounts deposited in the foreign currency
account and creditedin local currency to the budget management system. While no external audit will
be required it is expected that this written confirmation will be signed off by both the Accountant
General and the Auditor General, and will be supported by a credit note from the Central Bank. If the
proceeds of the credit are used for ineligible purposes (i.e. to finance goods or services on the standard
negative list), as defined inthe Financing Agreement, IDA will require the Borrower to promptly upon
notice from IDA, refundan amount equal to the amount of said payment to IDA. Amounts refunded to
the Bank upon such request shall be cancelled.          The administration of this credit will be the
responsibility of the Ministry of Finance. As required by law, the Auditor General will carry the audit
of public accounts and submit the annual report to the Parliament prior to the date of its first session
following the year of the review of these accounts by the General Audit Office. IDA will have access
to this report.


                                 F.      RISKSANDRISKMITIGATION

105.      The implementation of the poverty reduction strategy in Madagascar continues to be
exposed to risks, many of which were already identified in previous PRSC operations and the
CAS. The main risks include:

   Recent economic events may generate some social tensions.                  The political situation in
   Madagascar has been solidified further by the Presidential elections of December 2006 and
   municipal elections of December 2007. Nevertheless, social instability should not be discounted due
   to rising food and energy prices that have had a significant impact on the poorest households,
   especially in urban centers. Furthermore, recent massive foreign investments, albeit perceived as
   positive in the longer term, have increased pressures on policymakers to deal with short terms
   externalities such as environmental and social damages. The aftermath of the elections present an
    opportunity for the Government to deliver results which could ensure renewed vigor in
    implementing reforms and poverty-reduction programs. The Government has a clear vision of
   reform and is strongly committed to moving the MAP agenda forward. The Bank' continues to be
    responsive with a focus on issues of transparency and anti-corruption, with the PRSC and the
    Governance and Institutional Development Project being the main instruments of IDA support, and
    WBIplaying a key role through various governance and capacity programs.

    Madagascar remains vulnerable to external shocks, particularly cyclones and increases in
    global commodity prices, such as oil and rice. As detailed earlier, these shocks impact negatively
    on growth and poverty reduction. The Bank has supported the Government to finalize a Risk and
    Social Protection Review to assess risk prevention and mitigation strategies, and co-leading a Task
    Force to evaluate the damages from the latest cyclone inearly 2008. Initial steps have been taken to
    investigate the feasibility of specific options for risk mitigation to the external shocks faced by
    Madagascar (e.g., weather risk insurance schemes). The Bank has also modified procedures for its
    Community Development Project (FID IV) to allow it to respond more rapidly to emergencies such
    as cyclones; the FY05 supplemental IDA credit for the FID project was delivered inresponse to the
    two cyclones of 2004. The Bureau National de Gestion des Risques et des Catastropheshad its role
    and a competency formalized in December 2006 and i s in charged with managing programs to
    prevent crises and mitigate their impacts when they occur. Finally, the Government has made rice
    an important policy focus, with an emphasis on the entire rice value chain (production, distribution,
    marketing), and i s also looking at short-term policy options to better stabilize prices though fiscal
    measures, inclose collaborationwith the IMFand the World Bank.

    Maintaining a stable macroeconomic environment remains critical to the successful
    implementation of the M A P and the PRSC series. The Government made significant progress in
    2005 and 2006 in managing the high inflation in the face of continued high international prices.

                                                    41

Nevertheless, further inflationary pressures caused by world price volatility will continue to present
a challenge. Furthermore, the recent massive capital inflows have put some pressure on the real
exchange rate, which may discourage traditional export through the emergence o f the Dutch disease.
So far the Government has paid close attention to managing those macroeconomic effects by
adopting a prudent monetary policy, in line with the IMF PRGF. T o mitigate this risk further, the
Bank's investment portfolio supports Government's efforts to improve the competitiveness o f the
economy by adopting measures that increase productivity through building roads and other
infrastructure, improving governance, health, education, and PFM, facilitating private sector
development and thereby counterbalancing the potential negative impact o f the appreciation on
productive activities. While the budgetary impact o f higher oil prices i s under control in the short
term, critical to these efforts is the successful completion o f the restructuringplan o f the electric and
water power utility, JIRAMA, which i s a fundamental element for the maintenance o f a stable
macroeconomic environment given the negative repercussions on the whole economy o f the
persistent crisis. The Government, with support o f the Word Bank, has been working on a new
strategy for the energy sector that focuses on tariff adjustments (to bring them closer to real costs),
new investments to enhance (and diversify) production and distribution capabilities, and improve on
key performance through a long term management contract.

Capacity constraints present significant risks to effective implementation of reforms. Although
significant efforts have been undertaken by the Government to improve its internal capacity,
significant weaknesses remain especially at the sectoral and local levels. T o mitigate this risk, the
Government in close coordination with partner has prepared a capacity building strategy which is a
key element of its MAP. On capacity constraints, the donor community has intensified efforts at
coordinating assistance programs, harmonizing disbursement and procurement procedures and
developing common programmatic approaches around the MAP. The donor community, including
the bank through specific operations and assistance, has also increased its level o f technical
assistance and support for capacity building. Overall, the World Bank program continues to focus
on transparency and anti-corruption issues, with the PRSC and the Governance and Institutional
DevelopmentProject being the main instruments.

Potential delays in the implementation of public finance and structural reforms in strategic
sectors pose challenges and risks for improved basic service delivery. As detailed earlier,
Madagascar has carried out an ambitious set o f budgetary reforms that are already yielding results.
Nevertheless, a number of key issues are worrisome such as a weak fiscal framework that
overestimated revenues, unpredictable budget execution, and capacity weaknesses in applying the
new program budget structure, and poor reform coordination and M&E. There i s also a need to
solidify the implementation o f the new legal and institutional framework on procurement, which
would lead to significant cost savings and transparency in key sectors. Delays inreforms of priority
sectors, including energy, might lead to fiscal losses and slow down the development o f the private
sector. The Government has recognizedthese issues and has laid out a priority action plan for 2007
that continues and deepens the reform program. Successful implementation o f this priority action
plan will be critical to ensure real impact on service delivery and poverty reduction. The Bank
continues to support the reforms proposed in the areas of public finance, budget programming,
procurement, institutionaldevelopment and capacity buildingthrough the PRSC and the Governance
 and Institutional Development Project and an operation is planned in the energy sector to support
reforms and the implementation of a restructuring plan that should lead to private sector
participation in the medium-terms.        The Bank's on-going investment portfolio also focuses on
 institutional strengthening, training and capacity buildingat the sector level.

                      Annex 4: The World Bank Support to the Growth Agenda


1.       The PRSC series does not include specific actions that support Madagascar's growth
agenda because this pillar benefits from a strong support of the World Bank Group through a
series of analytical work and investment lendingoperations. It i s nonethelessworth underscoring that
the PRSC series provides support to the growth agenda by improving the budget process, the overall
governance environment and the human capital and general living standards of the population, which are
necessaryelements of a sound environment conducive to economic growth.

2.       This annex provides an overview of the existing Bank's program in support of the growth
agenda in Madagascar. It reviews recent accomplishments and summarizes the strategy set out in the
new CAS for future support as well as it describes the main activities of the World Bank Group insupport
of removing bottlenecks to investment and growth (Table 1 for a summary), with a focus on three key
areas: (i)private sector development, (ii)transport; and (iii)rural development and environment. The
main accomplishments of the last few years inthese three areas are summarized inTable 2.

Private Sector Development

3.        As mentioned inthe main text, the Bank has been active inconducting a series of analytical
studies with the objective to identify and remove constraints to private sector development. Among
those studies are the investment climate assessment realized in FY05, the DP/CEM that devoted to a
better understanding of the constraints to growth, and a series of analytical studies have been realized in
the context of the Integrated Trade Framework Initiative to promote regional integration and international
trade. IFC has also launched a few technical assistance programs to help local firms, notably SMES, to
get better access to information and training.

4.        Inparallel to these analytical studies, a series of investment lending operations have been
launched by the Bank, including the Integrated Growth Poles Project (IGP2) and Private Sector
Development Project 11. The newly established EDBM, an investment promotion and business
facilitation agency partially funded by the IGP2, has started to function as an apex body for private sector
development. The EDBMi s fully operational and has taken over the functions and responsibilities of the
former GUIDE for investment facilitation. As a result, there has been significant reduction in both the
time taken (21 days to 3 days) and the number of procedures required (10 to 3) for creating new
businesses. A new InvestmentLaw and amended Free Zone Law were adopted by the National Assembly
in December 2007 and are being implemented. To promote regional development, EDBM regional
offices have been established inNosy Be and Fort Dauphin to lead and coordinate efforts to improve the
sub-regional businessenvironment.

5.        The IG2P is expected to increase private investments in the three geographical growth
poles (Nosy Be, Antananarivo-Antsirabe, and Fort Dauphin) in a few specific key sectors of the
economy (mining, tourism, agribusiness, ICT, and textile). Along with infrastructure investments, the
IG2P provides support to improving the policy and regulatory regime and strengthening institutional
capacity to leverage private investments. The IG2P investments have triggered new investments in the
tourism and services sector inNosy Be and Fort Dauphin. The new Ehoala Port inFort Dauphin is under
construction and expected to be operational in early 2009 when the first mining exports from the Q M M
project are expected. Access to finance, under the joint IDA-IFC partial credit guarantee has improved
significantly with over US$18 million committed to MSMEs. Efforts are underway to concession tourism
sites inthe poles as well as ineco-tourism destinations.

6.        Inspite of recent steps taken to improve the business environment, significant constraints
remain especially in terms of poor access to reliable electricity. Most firms in rural areas have no
access to energy sources and those in urban areas suffer from shortages, lowering their competitiveness
on internationalmarkets. Although the Government is well aware of the situation, reforms in the energy

                                                    70

sector have been delayed over the past year and the JIRAMA situation remains critical. The authorities
have taken some measures, such as increases in electricity tariffs and adjustment costs, that should help
restore JIRAMA's financial situation in the short term. Over the medium term, a new strategy has been
agreed among the Government, the company's management and partners (including the World Bank) to
improve performance though a long term management contract.
